style="text-align:center;background-color: #efefef"><img alt="Lymphad3.svg" data-file-height="174" data-file-width="177" decoding="async" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Lymphad3.svg/25px-Lymphad3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Lymphad3.svg/39px-Lymphad3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Lymphad3.svg/51px-Lymphad3.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Shetland</b> (<a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>: <i lang="sco">Shetland</i>), also called the <b>Shetland Islands</b> and formerly <b>Zetland</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">subarctic</a> <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> of <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> that lies northeast of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>.\n</p><p>The islands lie some 80&#160;km (50&#160;mi) to the northeast of <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, 168&#160;km (104&#160;mi) from the Scottish mainland and 280&#160;km (170&#160;mi) southeast of the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>. They form part of the division between the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the west and the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> to the east. The total area is 1,466&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (566&#160;sq&#160;mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIC4_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIC4-1">[1]</a></sup> and the population totalled 23,210 in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Comprising the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_(Scottish_Parliament_constituency)" title="Shetland (Scottish Parliament constituency)">Shetland constituency</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands_Council" title="Shetland Islands Council">Shetland Islands Council</a> is one of the 32 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_areas" title="Council areas">council areas</a> of Scotland; the islands\' administrative centre and only <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burgh</a> is <a href="/wiki/Lerwick" title="Lerwick">Lerwick</a>, which has also been the capital of Shetland since taking over from <a href="/wiki/Scalloway" title="Scalloway">Scalloway</a> in 1708.\n</p><p>The largest island, known as the "<a href="/wiki/Mainland,_Shetland" title="Mainland, Shetland">Mainland</a>", has an area of 967&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (373&#160;sq&#160;mi), making it the third-largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Scotland" title="List of islands of Scotland">Scottish island</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_the_British_Isles_by_area" title="List of the British Isles by area">fifth-largest</a> of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>. There are an additional 15 inhabited islands. The archipelago has an <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a>, a complex geology, a rugged coastline and many low, rolling hills.\n</p><p>Humans have lived in Shetland since the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> period. The earliest written references to the islands date to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> times<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. The <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">early historic period</a> was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Norsemen" title="Norsemen">Scandinavian</a> influences, especially from <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, and the islands did not become part of Scotland until the 15th century. When Scotland became part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> in 1707, trade with northern Europe decreased. Fishing has continued to be an important aspect of the economy up to the present day. The discovery of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> in the 1970s significantly boosted Shetland\'s economy, employment and public sector revenues.\n</p><p>The local way of life reflects the Scottish and Norse heritage of the isles, including the <a href="/wiki/Up_Helly_Aa" title="Up Helly Aa">Up Helly Aa</a> fire festival, and a strong musical tradition, especially the traditional <a href="/wiki/Fiddle" title="Fiddle">fiddle</a> style. The islands have produced a variety of writers of prose and poetry, often in the distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shetland_dialect" title="Shetland dialect">Shetland dialect</a> of <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>. There are numerous areas set aside to protect the local <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flora" title="Flora">flora</a>, including a number of important sea bird nesting sites. The <a href="/wiki/Shetland_pony" title="Shetland pony">Shetland pony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Sheepdog" title="Shetland Sheepdog">Shetland Sheepdog</a> are two well-known <a href="/wiki/Shetland_animal_breeds" title="Shetland animal breeds">Shetland animal breeds</a>. Other local breeds include the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_sheep" title="Shetland sheep">Shetland sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shetland_cattle" title="Shetland cattle">cow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shetland_goose" title="Shetland goose">goose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shetland_duck" title="Shetland duck">duck</a>. The Shetland pig, or <a href="/wiki/Grice" title="Grice">grice</a>, has been extinct since about 1930.\n</p><p>The islands\' motto, which appears on the Council\'s <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a>, is "<i lang="is" title="Icelandic language text">Me&#240; l&#246;gum skal land byggja</i>". The <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> original of this <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> phrase is taken from the Danish 1241 Basic Law, <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Holmiensis" title="Codex Holmiensis">Code of Jutland</a></i>, and is also mentioned in <i><a href="/wiki/Nj%C3%A1ls_saga" title="Nj&#225;ls saga">Nj&#225;ls saga</a></i>, and means "By law shall land be built".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Geography_and_geology"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Geography and geology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Climate"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Climate</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Prehistory"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Prehistory</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Scandinavian_colonisation"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Scandinavian colonisation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 href="#18th_and_19th_centuries"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">18th and 19th centuries</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">20th century</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Economy"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Energy_and_fossil_fuels"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Energy and fossil fuels</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Farming_and_textiles"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Farming and textiles</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Media"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Media</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Tourism"><span class="tocnumber">6.5</span> <span class="toctext">Tourism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Quarries"><span class="tocnumber">6.6</span> <span class="toctext">Quarries</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Transport"><span class="tocnumber">6.7</span> <span class="toctext">Transport</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Public_services"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Public services</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Sport"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Sport</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Churches_and_religion"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Churches and religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Politics"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Shetland_flag"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Shetland flag</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Local_culture_and_the_arts"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Local culture and the arts</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Music"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">Music</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Writers"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Writers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Films_and_television"><span class="tocnumber">13.3</span> <span class="toctext">Films and television</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Wildlife"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Wildlife</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Flora"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Flora</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Fauna"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Fauna</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Domesticated_animals"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Domesticated animals</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Lists"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">Lists</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#About_Shetland"><span class="tocnumber">15.2</span> <span class="toctext">About Shetland</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-36"><a href="#Other"><span class="tocnumber">15.3</span> <span class="toctext">Other</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#General_references"><span class="tocnumber">17.1</span> <span class="toctext">General references</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">Northern Isles</a></div>\n<p>The name of Shetland is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> words, <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">hjalt</i> (\'<a href="/wiki/Hilt" title="Hilt">hilt</a>\'), and <i lang="non" title="Old Norse language text">land</i> (\'land\').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-&#229;_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-&#229;-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In AD 43 and 77 the Roman authors <a href="/wiki/Pomponius_Mela" title="Pomponius Mela">Pomponius Mela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> referred to the seven islands they respectively called <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Haemodae</i> and <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Acmodae</i>, both of which are assumed to be Shetland. Another possible early written reference to the islands is <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>\' report in <a href="/wiki/Agricola_(book)" title="Agricola (book)"><i>Agricola</i></a> in AD 98, after describing the discovery and conquest of Orkney, that the Roman fleet had seen "<a href="/wiki/Thule" title="Thule">Thule</a>, too".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[Note 1]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">early Irish</a> literature, Shetland is referred to as <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">Insi Catt</i>&#8212;"the Isles of Cats", which may have been the pre-Norse inhabitants\' name for the islands. The Cat tribe also occupied parts of the northern Scottish mainland and their name can be found in <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">Caithness</a>, and in the Gaelic name for <a href="/wiki/Sutherland" title="Sutherland">Sutherland</a> (<i lang="gd" title="Scottish Gaelic language text">Cataibh</i>, meaning "among the Cats").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[Note 2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The oldest version of the modern name Shetland is <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Hetlandensis</i>, the Latinised adjectival form of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> name recorded in a letter from Harald, Count of Shetland in 1190,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> becoming <i>Hetland</i> in 1431 after various intermediate transformations. It is possible that the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Pictish</a> "cat" sound forms part of this <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Norse</a> name. It then became <i lang="nrn" title="Norn language text">Hjaltland</i> in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G21_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-G21-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S9_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-S9-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[Note 3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As <a href="/wiki/Norn_language" title="Norn language">Norn</a> was gradually replaced by <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> in the form of the Shetland dialect, <i lang="nrn" title="Norn language text">Hjaltland</i> became <i lang="sco" title="Scots language text">&#540;etland</i>. The initial letter is the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Scots" title="Middle Scots">Middle Scots</a> letter, <i><a href="/wiki/Yogh" title="Yogh">yogh</a></i>, the pronunciation of which is almost identical to the original Norn sound, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/hj/</span>. When the use of the letter yogh was discontinued, it was often replaced by the similar-looking letter <a href="/wiki/Z" title="Z">z</a>, hence <i lang="sco" title="Scots language text">Zetland</i>, the form used in the name of the pre-1975 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_counties_of_Scotland" title="Traditional counties of Scotland">county council</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup> This is also the source of the <a href="/wiki/ZE_postcode_area" title="ZE postcode area">ZE</a> postcode used for Shetland.\n</p><p>Most of the individual islands have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_language" title="Norse language">Norse</a> names, although the derivations of some are obscure and may represent pre-Norse, possibly <a href="/wiki/Pictish_language" title="Pictish language">Pictish</a> or even pre-<a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a> names or elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_geology">Geography and geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography and geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Shetland_islands" title="List of Shetland islands">List of Shetland islands</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shetland_geology_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3543" data-file-width="2465" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Shetland_geology_map.png/200px-Shetland_geology_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Shetland_geology_map.png/300px-Shetland_geology_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Shetland_geology_map.png/400px-Shetland_geology_map.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shetland_geology_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shetland geological map</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Charlotte,_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Fort_Charlotte%2C_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg/220px-Fort_Charlotte%2C_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Fort_Charlotte%2C_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg/330px-Fort_Charlotte%2C_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Fort_Charlotte%2C_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg/440px-Fort_Charlotte%2C_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fort_Charlotte,_Lerwick_-_geograph.org.uk_-_17910.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fort_Charlotte,_Shetland" title="Fort Charlotte, Shetland">Fort Charlotte</a> overlooking <a href="/wiki/Lerwick" title="Lerwick">Lerwick</a>, Shetland\'s largest settlement.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Broch_of_Mousa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_2800.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="347" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Broch_of_Mousa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_2800.jpg/220px-Broch_of_Mousa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_2800.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Broch_of_Mousa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_2800.jpg/330px-Broch_of_Mousa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_2800.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Broch_of_Mousa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_2800.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Broch_of_Mousa_-_geograph.org.uk_-_2800.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Broch_of_Mousa" title="Broch of Mousa">Broch of Mousa</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Shetland is around 170 kilometres (110&#160;mi) north of mainland Scotland, covers an area of 1,468 square kilometres (567&#160;sq&#160;mi) and has a coastline 2,702 kilometres (1,679&#160;mi) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIC4_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-SIC4-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lerwick" title="Lerwick">Lerwick</a>, the capital and largest settlement, has a population of 6,958 and about half of the archipelago\'s total population of 23,167 people live within 16 kilometres (10&#160;mi) of the town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Scalloway" title="Scalloway">Scalloway</a> on the west coast, which was the capital until 1708, has a population of less than 1,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shetland_Islands_Council_2010_p._10_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shetland_Islands_Council_2010_p._10-21">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Only 16 of about 100 islands are inhabited. The main island of the group is known as <a href="/wiki/Mainland,_Shetland" title="Mainland, Shetland">Mainland</a>. The next largest are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yell_(island)" title="Yell (island)">Yell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unst" title="Unst">Unst</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fetlar" title="Fetlar">Fetlar</a>, which lie to the north, and <a href="/wiki/Bressay" title="Bressay">Bressay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whalsay" title="Whalsay">Whalsay</a>, which lie to the east. <a href="/wiki/East_Burra" title="East Burra">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Burra" title="West Burra">West Burra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muckle_Roe" title="Muckle Roe">Muckle Roe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papa_Stour" title="Papa Stour">Papa Stour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trondra" title="Trondra">Trondra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaila" title="Vaila">Vaila</a> are smaller islands to the west of Mainland. The other inhabited islands are <a href="/wiki/Foula" title="Foula">Foula</a> 28 kilometres (17&#160;mi) west of <a href="/wiki/Walls,_Shetland" title="Walls, Shetland">Walls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle" title="Fair Isle">Fair Isle</a> 38 kilometres (24&#160;mi) south-west of <a href="/wiki/Sumburgh_Head" title="Sumburgh Head">Sumburgh Head</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Out_Skerries" title="Out Skerries">Out Skerries</a> to the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[Note 4]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The uninhabited islands include <a href="/wiki/Mousa" title="Mousa">Mousa</a>, known for the <a href="/wiki/Broch_of_Mousa" title="Broch of Mousa">Broch of Mousa</a>, the finest preserved example in Scotland of an <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Broch" title="Broch">broch</a>; <a href="/wiki/Noss" title="Noss">Noss</a> to the east of <a href="/wiki/Bressay" title="Bressay">Bressay</a>, which has been a national nature reserve since 1955; <a href="/wiki/St_Ninian%27s_Isle" title="St Ninian\'s Isle">St Ninian\'s Isle</a>, connected to Mainland by the largest active <a href="/wiki/Tombolo" title="Tombolo">tombolo</a> in the UK; and <a href="/wiki/Out_Stack" title="Out Stack">Out Stack</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extreme_points_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Extreme points of the United Kingdom">northernmost</a> point of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OS_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-OS-24">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup> Shetland\'s location means that it provides a number of such records: <a href="/wiki/Muness_Castle" title="Muness Castle">Muness</a> is the most northerly castle in the United Kingdom and <a href="/wiki/Skaw,_Unst" title="Skaw, Unst">Skaw</a> the most northerly settlement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The geology of Shetland is complex, with numerous <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faults</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fold_(geology)#Describing_folds" title="Fold (geology)">fold axes</a>. These islands are the northern outpost of the <a href="/wiki/Caledonian_orogeny" title="Caledonian orogeny">Caledonian orogeny</a>, and there are outcrops of <a href="/wiki/Lewisian_complex" title="Lewisian complex">Lewisian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dalradian" title="Dalradian">Dalradian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moine_Supergroup" title="Moine Supergroup">Moine</a> metamorphic rocks with histories similar to their equivalents on the Scottish mainland. There are also <a href="/wiki/Old_Red_Sandstone" title="Old Red Sandstone">Old Red Sandstone</a> deposits and <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> intrusions. The most distinctive features are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultramafic" title="Ultramafic">ultrabasic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ophiolite" title="Ophiolite">ophiolite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peridotite" title="Peridotite">peridotite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gabbro" title="Gabbro">gabbro</a> on Unst and Fetlar, which are remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_Ocean" title="Iapetus Ocean">Iapetus Ocean</a> floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Much of Shetland\'s economy depends on the oil-bearing sediments in the surrounding seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup> Geological evidence shows that in around 6100 BC a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a> caused by the <a href="/wiki/Storegga_Slide" title="Storegga Slide">Storegga Slides</a> hit Shetland, as well as the rest of the east coast of Scotland, and may have created a wave of up to 25 metres (82&#160;ft) high in the <a href="/wiki/Firth" title="Firth">voes</a> where modern populations are highest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The highest point of Shetland is <a href="/wiki/Ronas_Hill" title="Ronas Hill">Ronas Hill</a> at 450 metres (1,480&#160;ft). The <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> glaciations entirely covered the islands. During that period, the Stanes of Stofast, a 2000-tonne <a href="/wiki/Glacial_erratic" title="Glacial erratic">glacial erratic</a>, came to rest on a prominent hilltop in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunnasting" title="Lunnasting">Lunnasting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Shetland is a <a href="/wiki/National_scenic_area_(Scotland)" title="National scenic area (Scotland)">national scenic area</a> which, unusually, includes a number of discrete locations: Fair Isle, Foula, South West Mainland (including the <a href="/wiki/Scalloway_Islands" title="Scalloway Islands">Scalloway Islands</a>), Muckle Roe, <a href="/wiki/Esha_Ness_Lighthouse" title="Esha Ness Lighthouse">Esha Ness</a>, Fethaland and <a href="/wiki/Hermaness" title="Hermaness">Herma Ness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> The total area covered by the designation is 41,833&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hectares" title="Hectares">ha</a>, of which 26,347&#160;ha is marine (i.e. below low tide).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2018, legislation came into force in Scotland to prevent public bodies, without good reason, showing Shetland in a separate box in maps, as had often been the practice.  The legislation requires the islands to be "displayed in a manner that accurately and proportionately represents their geographical location in relation to the rest of Scotland", so as make clear the islands\' real distance from other areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Shetland has an oceanic temperate maritime climate (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K&#246;ppen climate classification">K&#246;ppen</a>: <i>Cfb</i>), bordering on, but very slightly above average in summer temperatures, the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate#Subpolar_variety_(Cfc)" title="Oceanic climate">subpolar</a> variety, with long but cool winters and short mild summers. The climate all year round is moderate owing to the influence of the surrounding seas, with average night-time low temperatures a little above 1&#160;&#176;C (34&#160;&#176;F) in January and February and average daytime high temperatures of near 14&#160;&#176;C (57&#160;&#176;F) in July and August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-climatetemp_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-climatetemp-34">[30]</a></sup> The highest temperature on record was 23.4&#160;&#176;C (74.1&#160;&#176;F) in July 1991 and the lowest &#8722;8.9&#160;&#176;C (16.0&#160;&#176;F) in the Januaries of 1952 and 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIC200505_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIC200505-35">[31]</a></sup> The frost-free period may be as little as three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup> In contrast, inland areas of nearby <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> on similar latitudes experience significantly larger temperature differences between summer and winter, with the average highs of regular July days comparable to Lerwick\'s all-time record heat that is around 23&#160;&#176;C (73&#160;&#176;F), further demonstrating the moderating effect of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>. In contrast, winters are considerably milder than those expected in nearby continental areas, even comparable to winter temperatures of many parts of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> much further south.\n</p><p>The general character of the climate is windy and cloudy with at least 2&#160;mm (0.08&#160;in) of rain falling on more than 250 days a year. Average yearly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> is 1,003&#160;mm (39.5&#160;in), with November and December the wettest months. Snowfall is usually confined to the period November to February, and snow seldom lies on the ground for more than a day. Less rain falls from April to August although no month receives less than 50&#160;mm (2&#160;in). <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">Fog</a> is common during summer due to the cooling effect of the sea on mild southerly airflows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-climatetemp_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-climatetemp-34">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIC200505_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-SIC200505-35">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of the islands\' <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, on clear winter nights the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_borealis" title="Aurora borealis">"northern lights"</a> can sometimes be seen in the sky, while in summer there is almost <a href="/wiki/Twilight#Duration" title="Twilight">perpetual daylight</a>, a state of affairs known locally as the "simmer dim".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> Annual bright sunshine averages 1110 hours, and overcast days are common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:100%; color:#FFFFFF;">68.8\n</td>\n<td style="background: #3F3F3F; color:#FFFFFF;">33.8\n</td>\n<td style="background: #212121; color:#FFFFFF;">18.1\n</td>\n<td style="background: #AAAAA6; color:#000000; border-left-width:medium">1,109.9\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="14" style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;">Source: <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Shetland" title="Prehistoric Shetland">Prehistoric Shetland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="641" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg/220px-Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg/330px-Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg/440px-Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jarlshof_20080821_-_aisled_roundhouse_and_broch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The preserved ruins of a <a href="/wiki/Wheelhouse_(archaeology)" title="Wheelhouse (archaeology)">wheelhouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Broch" title="Broch">broch</a> at <a href="/wiki/Jarlshof" title="Jarlshof">Jarlshof</a>, described as "one of the most remarkable archaeological sites ever excavated in the British Isles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jarl_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-jarl-41">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Due to the practice, dating to at least the early Neolithic, of building in stone on virtually treeless islands, Shetland is extremely rich in physical remains of the prehistoric eras and there are over 5,000 archaeological sites all told.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Midden" title="Midden">midden</a> site at West Voe on the south coast of Mainland, dated to 4320&#8211;4030 BC, has provided the first evidence of Mesolithic human activity on Shetland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup> The same site provides dates for early <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> activity and finds at <a href="/wiki/Scord_of_Brouster" title="Scord of Brouster">Scord of Brouster</a> in <a href="/wiki/Walls,_Shetland" title="Walls, Shetland">Walls</a> have been dated to 3400 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[Note 5]</a></sup> "Shetland knives" are stone tools that date from this period made from <a href="/wiki/Felsite" title="Felsite">felsite</a> from <a href="/wiki/Northmavine" title="Northmavine">Northmavine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch10_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch10-47">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pottery shards found at the important site of <a href="/wiki/Jarlshof" title="Jarlshof">Jarlshof</a> also indicate that there was Neolithic activity there although the main settlement dates from the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup> This includes a <a href="/wiki/Forge" title="Forge">smithy</a>, a cluster of <a href="/wiki/Wheelhouse_(archaeology)" title="Wheelhouse (archaeology)">wheelhouses</a> and a later broch. The site has provided evidence of habitation during various phases right up until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Viking</a> times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jarl_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-jarl-41">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heel-shaped_cairn" title="Heel-shaped cairn">Heel-shaped cairns</a>, are a style of <a href="/wiki/Chambered_cairn" title="Chambered cairn">chambered cairn</a> unique to Shetland, with a particularly large example on <a href="/wiki/Vementry" title="Vementry">Vementry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch10_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sch10-47">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Numerous brochs were erected during the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>. In addition to Mousa there are significant ruins at <a href="/wiki/Broch_of_Clickimin" title="Broch of Clickimin">Clickimin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Broch_of_Culswick" title="Broch of Culswick">Culswick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Scatness" title="Old Scatness">Old Scatness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Broch_of_West_Burrafirth" title="Broch of West Burrafirth">West Burrafirth</a>, although their origin and purpose is a matter of some controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup> The later Iron Age inhabitants of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">Northern Isles</a> were probably Pictish, although the historical record is sparse. Hunter (2000) states in relation to King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bridei_I_of_the_Picts" title="Bridei I of the Picts">Bridei I of the Picts</a> in the sixth century AD: "As for Shetland, Orkney, Skye and the Western Isles, their inhabitants, most of whom appear to have been Pictish in culture and speech at this time, are likely to have regarded Bridei as a fairly distant presence.&#8221;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup> In 2011, the collective site, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Crucible_of_Iron_Age_Shetland" title="The Crucible of Iron Age Shetland">The Crucible of Iron Age Shetland</a>", including Broch of Mousa, Old Scatness and Jarlshof, joined the UKs "Tentative List" of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites_in_Scotland" title="World Heritage Sites in Scotland">World Heritage Sites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC311_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC311-53">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Shetland" title="History of Shetland">History of Shetland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scandinavian_colonisation">Scandinavian colonisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scandinavian colonisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shetland_(boxed)_with_surrounding_lands.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="773" data-file-width="972" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Shetland_%28boxed%29_with_surrounding_lands.png/220px-Shetland_%28boxed%29_with_surrounding_lands.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Shetland_%28boxed%29_with_surrounding_lands.png/330px-Shetland_%28boxed%29_with_surrounding_lands.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Shetland_%28boxed%29_with_surrounding_lands.png/440px-Shetland_%28boxed%29_with_surrounding_lands.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shetland_(boxed)_with_surrounding_lands.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shetland (boxed) in relation to surrounding territories including <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> (to the east), the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> (to the north west), and <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and the rest of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> (to the south west)</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg"><img alt="A page from an illuminated manuscript shows two male figures. On the left a seated man wears a red crown and on the right a standing man has long fair hair. Their right hands are clasped together." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg/220px-Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg/330px-Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg/440px-Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flateyjarbok_Haraldr_Halfdan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>14th-century <i><a href="/wiki/Flateyjarb%C3%B3k" title="Flateyjarb&#243;k">Flateyjarb&#243;k</a></i> image of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harald_I_of_Norway" title="Harald I of Norway">Harald H&#229;rfagre</a>, who took control of <i lang="nrn" title="Norn language text">Hjaltland</i> c. 875.</div></div></div>\n<p>The expanding population of <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a> led to a shortage of available resources and arable land there and led to a period of <a href="/wiki/Viking_expansion" title="Viking expansion">Viking expansion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_peoples" title="North Germanic peoples">Norse</a> gradually shifting their attention from plundering to invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> Shetland was colonised during the late 8th and 9th centuries,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup> the fate of the existing indigenous population being uncertain. Modern Shetlanders have almost identical proportions of Scandinavian matrilineal and patrilineal genetic ancestry, suggesting that the islands were settled by both men and women in equal measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodacre_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodacre-56">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> then used the islands as a base for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pirate" title="Pirate">pirate</a> expeditions to Norway and the coasts of mainland Scotland. In response, Norwegian king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harald_I_of_Norway" title="Harald I of Norway">Harald H&#229;rfagre</a> ("Harald Fair Hair") annexed the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">Northern Isles</a> (comprising Orkney and Shetland) in 875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[Note 6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rognvald_Eysteinsson" title="Rognvald Eysteinsson">Rognvald Eysteinsson</a> received Orkney and Shetland from Harald as an earldom as reparation for the death of his son in battle in Scotland, and then passed the earldom on to his brother <a href="/wiki/Sigurd_Eysteinsson" title="Sigurd Eysteinsson">Sigurd the Mighty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The islands converted to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> in the late 10th century. King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olaf_I_of_Norway" title="Olaf I of Norway">Olav Tryggvasson</a> summoned the <i><a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">jarl</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Sigurd_the_Stout" title="Sigurd the Stout">Sigurd the Stout</a> during a visit to Orkney and said, "I order you and all your subjects to be baptised. If you refuse, I\'ll have you killed on the spot and I swear I will ravage every island with fire and steel." Unsurprisingly, Sigurd agreed and the islands became Christian at a stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-T69_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-T69-60">[54]</a></sup> Unusually, from c. 1100 onwards the Norse <i>jarls</i> owed allegiance both to Norway and to the Scottish crown through their holdings as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earls_of_Caithness" title="Earls of Caithness">Earls of Caithness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1194, when <a href="/wiki/Harald_Maddadsson" title="Harald Maddadsson">Harald Maddadsson</a> was <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Orkney" title="Earl of Orkney">Earl of Orkney and Shetland</a>, a rebellion broke out against King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sverre_Sigurdsson" title="Sverre Sigurdsson">Sverre Sigurdsson</a> of Norway. The <i lang="nrn" title="Norn language text">&#216;yskjeggs</i> ("Island Beardies") sailed for Norway but were beaten in the <a href="/wiki/Sigurd_Magnusson#Battle_of_Florv&#229;g" title="Sigurd Magnusson">Battle of Florv&#229;g</a> near <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a>. After his victory King Sverre placed Shetland under direct Norwegian rule, a state of affairs that continued for nearly two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch13_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch13-62">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_Scottish_interest">Increased Scottish interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Increased Scottish interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From the mid-13th century onwards Scottish monarchs increasingly sought to take control of the islands surrounding the mainland. The process was begun in earnest by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Scotland" title="Alexander II of Scotland">Alexander II</a> and was continued by his successor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a>. This strategy eventually led to an invasion of Scotland by <a href="/wiki/Haakon_IV_of_Norway" title="Haakon IV of Norway">Haakon Haakonsson</a>, King of Norway. His fleet assembled in Bressay Sound before sailing for Scotland. After the stalemate of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Largs" title="Battle of Largs">Battle of Largs</a>, Haakon retreated to Orkney, where he died in December 1263, entertained on his deathbed by recitations of the sagas. His death halted any further Norwegian expansion in Scotland and following this ill-fated expedition, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mann_and_the_Isles" title="Kingdom of Mann and the Isles">Hebrides and Mann</a> were yielded to the Kingdom of Scotland as a result of the 1266 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a>, although the Scots recognised continuing Norwegian sovereignty over Orkney and Shetland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annexation_by_Scotland">Annexation by Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Annexation by Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_III_and_Margaret_of_Denmark.jpg"><img alt="A picture on a page in an old book. A man at left wears tights and a tunic with a lion rampant design and holds a sword and sceptre. A woman at right wears a dress with an heraldic design bordered with ermine and carries a thistle in one hand and a sceptre in the other. They stand on a green surface over a legend in Scots that begins &quot;James the Thrid of Nobil Memorie...&quot; (sic) and notes that he &quot;marrit the King of Denmark\'s dochter.&quot;" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="346" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/James_III_and_Margaret_of_Denmark.jpg/220px-James_III_and_Margaret_of_Denmark.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/James_III_and_Margaret_of_Denmark.jpg/330px-James_III_and_Margaret_of_Denmark.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/James_III_and_Margaret_of_Denmark.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_III_and_Margaret_of_Denmark.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a> and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Denmark,_Queen_of_Scotland" title="Margaret of Denmark, Queen of Scotland">Margaret</a>, whose betrothal led to Shetland passing from Norway to Scotland</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 14th century, Orkney and Shetland remained a Norwegian possession, but Scottish influence was growing. <a href="/wiki/Jon_Haraldsson" title="Jon Haraldsson">Jon Haraldsson</a>, who was murdered in <a href="/wiki/Thurso" title="Thurso">Thurso</a> in 1231, was the last of an unbroken line of Norse jarls,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup> and thereafter the earls were Scots noblemen of the houses of <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Angus" title="Earl of Angus">Angus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_Sinclair,_Earl_of_Orkney" title="Henry I Sinclair, Earl of Orkney">St Clair</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[62]</a></sup> On the death of <a href="/wiki/Haakon_VI_of_Norway" title="Haakon VI of Norway">Haakon VI</a> in 1380,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[63]</a></sup> Norway formed a <a href="/wiki/Kalmar_Union" title="Kalmar Union">political union</a> with Denmark, after which the interest of the royal house in the islands declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch13_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sch13-62">[56]</a></sup> In 1469, Shetland was <a href="/wiki/Pledge_(law)" title="Pledge (law)">pledged</a> by <a href="/wiki/Christian_I_of_Denmark" title="Christian I of Denmark">Christian I</a>, in his capacity as King of Norway, as security against the payment of the <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a> of his daughter <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Denmark,_Queen_of_Scotland" title="Margaret of Denmark, Queen of Scotland">Margaret</a>, betrothed to <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III of Scotland</a>. As the money was never paid, the connection with the Crown of Scotland became permanent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[Note 7]</a></sup> In 1470, <a href="/wiki/William_Sinclair,_1st_Earl_of_Caithness" title="William Sinclair, 1st Earl of Caithness">William Sinclair, 1st Earl of Caithness</a> ceded his title to James III, and the following year the Northern Isles were directly annexed to the Crown of Scotland,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> an action confirmed by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> in 1472.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Shetland\'s connection with Norway has proved to be enduring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[Note 8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the early 15th century on the Shetlanders sold their goods through the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> of German merchantmen. The Hansa would buy shiploads of salted fish, wool and butter, and import <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloth" title="Cloth">cloth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and other goods. The late 16th century and early 17th century were dominated by the influence of the despotic <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stewart,_1st_Earl_of_Orkney" title="Robert Stewart, 1st Earl of Orkney">Robert Stewart</a>, Earl of Orkney, who was granted the islands by his half-sister <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary Queen of Scots</a>, and his son <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Stewart,_2nd_Earl_of_Orkney" title="Patrick Stewart, 2nd Earl of Orkney">Patrick</a>. The latter commenced the building of <a href="/wiki/Scalloway_Castle" title="Scalloway Castle">Scalloway Castle</a>, but after his imprisonment in 1609 the Crown annexed Orkney and Shetland again until 1643 when <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> granted them to <a href="/wiki/William_Douglas,_7th_Earl_of_Morton" title="William Douglas, 7th Earl of Morton">William Douglas, 7th Earl of Morton</a>. These rights were held on and off by the Mortons until 1766, when they were sold by <a href="/wiki/James_Douglas,_14th_Earl_of_Morton" title="James Douglas, 14th Earl of Morton">James Douglas, 14th Earl of Morton</a> to Laurence Dundas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_and_19th_centuries">18th and 19th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 18th and 19th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2055" data-file-width="2850" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg/220px-Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg/330px-Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg/440px-Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wind_Bound_Lerwick_around_1880.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Full-rigged ship <i>Maella</i>, of <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a>, in Bressay Sound, around 1922</div></div></div>\n<p>The trade with the North German towns lasted until the 1707 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1707" title="Act of Union 1707">Act of Union</a>, when high salt duties prevented the German merchants from trading with Shetland. Shetland then went into an economic depression, as the local traders were not as skilled in trading salted fish. However, some local merchant-lairds took up where the German merchants had left off, and fitted out their own ships to export fish from Shetland to the Continent. For the independent farmers of Shetland this had negative consequences, as they now had to fish for these merchant-lairds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Visit.Shetland.org_history_page_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Visit.Shetland.org_history_page-78">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">Smallpox</a> afflicted the islands in the 17th and 18th centuries (as it did all of Europe), but as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox_vaccine" title="Smallpox vaccine">vaccines</a> became available after 1800, health improved. The island was very badly hit by the <a href="/wiki/Highland_Potato_Famine" title="Highland Potato Famine">potato famine of 1846</a> and the government introduced a Relief Plan for the island under the command of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rear_Admiral_Robert_Craigie" title="Rear Admiral Robert Craigie">Captain Robert Craigie</a> of the Royal Navy who stayed in Lerwick to oversee the project 1847-1852. During this period Craigie also did much to improve and increase roads on the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Population increased to a maximum of 31,670 in 1861. However, British rule came at price for many ordinary people as well as traders. The Shetlanders\' nautical skills were sought by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>. Some 3,000 served during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleonic_wars" title="Napoleonic wars">Napoleonic wars</a> from 1800 to 1815 and <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">press gangs</a> were rife. During this period 120 men were taken from Fetlar alone, and only 20 of them returned home. By the late 19th century 90% of all Shetland was owned by just 32 people, and between 1861 and 1881 more than 8,000 Shetlanders emigrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch16_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sch16-81">[73]</a></sup> With the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Crofters%27_Holdings_(Scotland)_Act_1886" title="Crofters\' Holdings (Scotland) Act 1886">Crofters\' Act</a> in 1886 the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> prime minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Gladstone</a> emancipated crofters from the rule of the landlords. The Act enabled those who had effectively been landowners\' serfs to become owner-occupiers of their own small farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup> By this time fishermen from Holland, who had traditionally gathered each year off the coast of Shetland to fish for <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, triggered an industry in the islands that boomed from around 1880 until the 1920s when stocks of the fish began to dwindle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[75]</a></sup> The production peaked in 1905 at more than a million barrels, of which 708,000 were exported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leif_Larsen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="411" data-file-width="357" decoding="async" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Leif_Larsen.jpg/220px-Leif_Larsen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Leif_Larsen.jpg/330px-Leif_Larsen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/Leif_Larsen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leif_Larsen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Leif_Larsen" title="Leif Larsen">Leif "Shetland" Larsen</a>, Norwegian leader of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shetland_Bus" title="Shetland Bus">Shetland Bus</a> operations in World War II, the most highly decorated <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">allied</a> naval officer of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scotsatwar_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-scotsatwar-85">[77]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> many Shetlanders served in the <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Highlanders" title="Gordon Highlanders">Gordon Highlanders</a>, a further 3,000 served in the Merchant Navy, and more than 1,500 in a special local naval reserve. The 10th Cruiser Squadron was stationed at Swarbacks Minn (the stretch of water to the south of Muckle Roe), and during a single year from March 1917 more than 4,500 ships sailed from Lerwick as part of an escorted convoy system. In total, Shetland lost more than 500 men, a higher proportion than any other part of Britain, and there were further waves of emigration in the 1920s and 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sch16_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sch16-81">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N94_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-N94-86">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> a Norwegian naval unit nicknamed the "<a href="/wiki/Shetland_bus" title="Shetland bus">Shetland Bus</a>" was established by the <a href="/wiki/Special_Operations_Executive" title="Special Operations Executive">Special Operations Executive</a> in the autumn of 1940 with a base first at <a href="/wiki/Lunna_House" title="Lunna House">Lunna</a> and later in Scalloway to conduct operations around the coast of Norway. About 30 fishing vessels used by Norwegian refugees were gathered and the Shetland Bus conducted covert operations, carrying intelligence agents, refugees, instructors for the resistance, and military supplies. It made over 200 trips across the sea, and <a href="/wiki/Leif_Larsen" title="Leif Larsen">Leif Larsen</a>, the most highly decorated <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">allied</a> naval officer of the war, made 52 of them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scotsatwar_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-scotsatwar-85">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[79]</a></sup> Several RAF airfields and sites were also established at Sullom Voe and several lighthouses suffered enemy air attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-N94_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-N94-86">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Oil reserves discovered in the later 20th century in the seas both east and west of Shetland have provided a much-needed alternative source of income for the islands. The <a href="/wiki/East_Shetland_Basin" title="East Shetland Basin">East Shetland Basin</a> is one of Europe\'s largest oil fields and as a result of the oil revenue and the cultural links with Norway, a small <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Movement" title="Shetland Movement">Home Rule movement</a> developed briefly to recast the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constitutional_position_of_Shetland" title="Constitutional position of Shetland">constitutional position of Shetland</a>. It saw as its models the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, as well as Shetland\'s closest neighbour, the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, an autonomous dependency of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[80]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maquereaux_etal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Maquereaux_etal.jpg/220px-Maquereaux_etal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Maquereaux_etal.jpg/330px-Maquereaux_etal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Maquereaux_etal.jpg/440px-Maquereaux_etal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maquereaux_etal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>More than half of the Shetland catch by weight and value is <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">mackerel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIC16_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-SIC16-89">[81]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Today, the main revenue producers in Shetland are agriculture, <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Scotland" title="Renewable energy in Scotland">renewable energy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum industry</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crude_oil" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> production), the creative industries and tourism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fishing">Fishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Fishing remains central to the islands\' economy today, with the total catch being 75,767 tonnes (74,570 long tons; 83,519 short tons) in 2009, valued at over &#163;73.2 million. <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">Mackerel</a> makes up more than half of the catch in Shetland by weight and value, and there are significant landings of <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">cod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_herring" title="Atlantic herring">herring</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merlangius_merlangus" title="Merlangius merlangus">whiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lophius_piscatorius" title="Lophius piscatorius">monkfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIC16_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-SIC16-89">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_and_fossil_fuels">Energy and fossil fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Energy and fossil fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg/220px-Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg/330px-Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg/440px-Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beryl_alpha_from_air.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Apache_Corporation" title="Apache Corporation">Apache Corporation</a>\'s Beryl alpha oil platform in the <a href="/wiki/East_Shetland_Basin" title="East Shetland Basin">East Shetland Basin</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Oil and gas were first landed in 1978 at <a href="/wiki/Sullom_Voe" title="Sullom Voe">Sullom Voe</a>, which has subsequently become one of the largest terminals in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup> Taxes from the oil have increased public sector spending on social welfare, art, sport, environmental measures and financial development. Three quarters of the islands\' workforce is employed in the service sector,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[85]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands_Council" title="Shetland Islands Council">Shetland Islands Council</a> alone accounted for 27.9% of output in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup> Shetland\'s access to oil revenues has funded the Shetland Charitable Trust, which in turn funds a wide variety of local programmes. The balance of the fund in 2011 was &#163;217 million, i.e., about &#163;9,500 per head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[Note 9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2007, the Shetland Islands Council signed a partnership agreement with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_and_Southern_Energy" title="Scottish and Southern Energy">Scottish and Southern Energy</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Wind_Farm" title="Viking Wind Farm">Viking Wind Farm</a>, a 200-turbine <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farm</a> and subsea cable. This <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> project would produce about 600 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">megawatts</a> and contribute about &#163;20 million to the Shetland economy per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[90]</a></sup> The plan met with significant opposition within the islands, primarily resulting from the anticipated visual impact of the development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[91]</a></sup> The PURE project on Unst is a research centre which uses a combination of wind power and <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cells</a> to create a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind-Hydrogen_Hybrid_Power_Systems" title="Wind-Hydrogen Hybrid Power Systems">wind hydrogen</a> system. The project is run by the Unst Partnership, the local community\'s <a href="/wiki/Development_trust" title="Development trust">development trust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wfm_shetland_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2624" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Wfm_shetland_map.png/220px-Wfm_shetland_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Wfm_shetland_map.png/330px-Wfm_shetland_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Wfm_shetland_map.png/440px-Wfm_shetland_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wfm_shetland_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shetland</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farming_and_textiles">Farming and textiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Farming and textiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Farming is mostly concerned with the raising of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shetland_(sheep)" title="Shetland (sheep)">Shetland sheep</a>, known for their unusually fine wool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shetland_Islands_Council_2010_p._10_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shetland_Islands_Council_2010_p._10-21">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[95]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Knitwear is important both to the economy and culture of Shetland, and the <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle_(technique)" title="Fair Isle (technique)">Fair Isle design</a> is well known. However, the industry faces challenges due to <a href="/wiki/Plagiarism" title="Plagiarism">plagiarism</a> of the word "Shetland" by manufacturers operating elsewhere, and a certification trademark, "The Shetland Lady", has been registered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Crofting" title="Crofting">Crofting</a>, the farming of small plots of land on a legally restricted tenancy basis, is still practised and is viewed as a key Shetland tradition as well as an important source of income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[97]</a></sup> Crops raised include oats and barley; however, the cold, windswept islands make for a harsh environment for most plants.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Shetland is served by a weekly local newspaper, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Shetland_Times" title="The Shetland Times">The Shetland Times</a></i> and the online <i>Shetland News</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[98]</a></sup>  with radio service being provided by <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Shetland" title="BBC Radio Shetland">BBC Radio Shetland</a> and the commercial radio station <a href="/wiki/SIBC" title="SIBC">SIBC</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Shetland is a popular destination for cruise ships, and in 2010 the <a href="/wiki/Lonely_Planet" title="Lonely Planet">Lonely Planet</a> guide named Shetland as the sixth best region in the world for tourists seeking unspoilt destinations. The islands were described as "beautiful and rewarding" and the Shetlanders as "a fiercely independent and self-reliant bunch".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[100]</a></sup> Overall visitor expenditure was worth &#163;16.4 million in 2006, in which year just under 26,000 cruise liner passengers arrived at Lerwick Harbour. This business has grown substantially with 109 cruise ships already booked in for 2019, representing over 107,000 passenger visits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[101]</a></sup> In 2009, the most popular visitor attractions were the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Museum" title="Shetland Museum">Shetland Museum</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RSPB" title="RSPB">RSPB</a> reserve at Sumburgh Head, Bonhoga Gallery at <a href="/wiki/Weisdale_Mill" title="Weisdale Mill">Weisdale Mill</a> and Jarlshof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quarries">Quarries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Shetland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Quarries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>Brindister: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Shetland&amp;params=60.114475_N_1.215874_W_"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">60&#176;06&#8242;52&#8243;N</span> <span class="longitude">1&#176;12&#8242;57&#8243;W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#65279;
